---

## Pass 5 — dedicated performance audit (easy wins + noticeable problems)

> **Status / provenance.** This pass was **stopped early at the user's request.** 21 of 27 hot-path finders completed (emitting these candidates); the adversarial verify stage had only just begun (4 of ~37 verdicts, 2 confirmed / 2 rejected). **In follow-ups (Pass 5b–5f) I hand-verified 31 more against source — all confirmed REAL, though many of the easy wins are low/negligible micro-optimizations and a few carry fix caveats (e.g. `InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:183` — the suggested `Tuple(generator)` fix is itself type-unstable). Together with the 2 ⭐ spot-checks: 33 verified total, each flagged ✅/⚠️ below.** The remaining ~4 (all cross-referenced above: `linearperiodfilling.jl:211/240/159`, `ITime.jl:335`) are effectively covered by sibling findings; treat as strong leads. The two ⭐high-impact items were also hand-verified against source. All were deduped by the finders against the 9 perf findings already in this doc. Scope was strictly HOT paths (per-timestep `evaluate!`/`regrid`/`read`/interpolation and the LRU cache); constructor/setup work was excluded. No Julia executed.

**Collected: 37 candidates** — 20 easy wins (≤ a few lines), 17 noticeable/structural. By impact: 2 high, 16 medium, 19 low.

### Noticeable / structural performance problems

_(bigger than a one-liner — may need a preallocated buffer, a struct field, an algorithm change, or a type-stability fix)_

- ⭐ **`ext/NCFileReaderExt.jl:310`** _(impact: high, effort: moderate)_ ✅spot-verified — **The in-place read! allocates a full grid-sized temporary array (and, on cache hits, a wasted copy) every timestep instead of reading/copying directly into dest.** (in `FileReaders.read!(dest, file_reader, date)`) **Mechanism:** read!(dest, fr, date) is implemented as `dest .= FileReaders.read(fr, date)`. read(fr, date) never writes into dest: on a cache hit it returns `copy(_cached_reads[date])` (a fresh grid-sized allocation, L237), and on a miss it materializes the disk slice and then broadcasts `preprocess_func.(...)` into yet another new array (L259-261). read! then copies that temporary into dest with `.=`. So the intermediate grid-sized array is pure overhead: for cache hits the `copy` is entirely wasted (could be `dest .= _cached_reads[date]`), and for misses the slice could be broadcast straight into dest. **Why hot:** read!(dest, ...) is the in-place per-timestep API whose entire purpose is to avoid allocation, yet it heap-allocates one grid-sized array (the whole lon/lat field, e.g. ~720x360 ~= 260k elements, ~2MB) on every single call, plus an extra memcpy. This runs once per read per timestep for time-varying inputs. **Fix:** Give read! its own implementation that writes into dest without the intermediate: for a cache hit do `dest .= file_reader._cached_reads[date]` (or `dest .= preprocess_func.(cached)`); for a miss compute the slice once and do `dest .= preprocess_func.(slice)` directly into dest (optionally still populating the cache). Factor the shared slice-locating logic into a helper so both read and read! call it without forcing an owned-copy allocation on the read! path.
- ⭐ **`ext/TempestRegridderExt.jl:114`** _(impact: high, effort: moderate)_ ✅spot-verified — **regrid has no internal memoization: every call reopens the per-date HDF5 file and re-reads the full grid-sized field from disk, so repeated same-date calls redo 100% identical disk I/O + allocation.** (in `Regridders.regrid(regridder::TempestRegridder, date)`) **Mechanism:** regrid unconditionally forwards to read_from_hdf5 (114), which builds a path (208), constructs a new ClimaCore.InputOutput.HDF5Reader (212 - opens the file and parses its metadata), reads the whole field (214), and closes (215). There is no state on the regridder to remember the last (date -> field) result, so calling regrid twice for the same date does the entire open+parse+read+alloc twice. **Why hot:** regrid is the documented per-date public entry point (docstring 108-111) and is explicitly a hot path (called per coupling/timestep). When used directly (TempestRegridder predates DataHandler and is driven per-step in ClimaCoupler), each timestep reopens the HDF5 file and materializes a grid-sized Field (order Nelem*Nq^2*Nfields Float64, ~1e5-1e6 values on a cubed sphere) for data that never changes for a fixed date. The only thing preventing this today is an external cache in DataHandlingExt.jl:604, which does not protect the standalone regrid API and is a bounded LRU that re-reads on eviction. **Fix:** Give the regridder its own small memoization: add a mutable cache field (e.g. a Ref/Dict or a tiny LRU of date=>Field) to the TempestRegridder struct (or wrap it), and in regrid/read_from_hdf5 return the cached field on a hit instead of reopening/reading. This is a struct-signature change plus a get!-style guard in regrid.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:163`** _(impact: medium, effort: large)_ — **Derived horizontal LatLong coordinate Field is rebuilt over every grid point on every regrid, even though the coordinate transform is invariant across time samples.** (in `Regridders.regrid`) **Mechanism:** When the regridder's coordinates are LatLongZPoint but 2D data is regridded (length(dimensions)==2, non-extruded space), `coords = map(regridder.coordinates) do coord; LatLongPoint(coord.lat, coord.long) end` allocates a brand-new grid-sized coordinate Field each call. `regridder.coordinates` never changes between calls, so the mapped result is identical every time; it is pure per-call recomputation of an invariant. **Why hot:** regrid is invoked once per cache-miss (per new time sample) inside regridded_snapshot's get! closure (DataHandlingExt.jl:634). This branch allocates a Field sized by the number of horizontal grid points (thousands to millions on a real target space) on every such call, versus a scalar/constant cost if precomputed once. **Fix:** Precompute the horizontal-only coordinate Field once and reuse it. Either add a memoizing cache field (e.g. a Ref/Dict keyed by the needed point type) to InterpolationsRegridder that is populated on first use, or have the DataHandler construct a regridder whose `coordinates` already match the data dimensionality, so `regrid` can use `regridder.coordinates` directly instead of rebuilding the LatLongPoint field each call. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5d):** REAL, large, medium. Confirmed the branch (LatLongZ coords + non-extruded target + 2D data, L155-163) rebuilds a horizontal coordinate Field via `map(regridder.coordinates)` on every regrid; `regridder.coordinates` is fixed at construction, so the mapped Field (one point per horizontal grid point — thousands to millions) is a grid-sized allocation recomputed each call. Precompute/cache-once is a valid win. Only fires for the LatLongZ + 2D-data case, but per-timestep when it does.
- **`ext/NCFileReaderExt.jl:237`** _(impact: medium, effort: large)_ — **The LRU read cache is typed with an abstract element type (LRUCache{DateTime, Array}), so every cache hit returns an abstractly-typed Array{T,N} where {T,N}, making read/read! type-unstable on the hot path.** (in `FileReaders.read(file_reader, date)`) **Mechanism:** The `_cached_reads` field (L64) is constrained as `CACHE <: LRUCache{DateTime, <:AbstractArray}` and is instantiated at L176 with the abstract value type `Array` (i.e. `Array{T,N} where {T,N}`, no eltype/ndims). Consequently `file_reader._cached_reads[date]` (L237) and the `get!` results (L242, L283) are inferred as abstract `Array`, and `copy(...)` of that is likewise abstract. The return type of `read(file_reader, date)` therefore cannot be inferred to a concrete array, forcing dynamic dispatch (and potential boxing) at every call site that consumes the read result, including the `dest .= read(...)` broadcast in read! and downstream regrid/interpolation. **Why hot:** Runs on every per-timestep read and read! (and propagates instability to regridded_snapshot!/evaluate! callers): the array element/dim type is unknown at compile time, so the returned field is handled through dynamic dispatch every step rather than as a concrete Array{FT,N}. **Fix:** Parameterize the cache with a concrete array type at construction, e.g. determine the post-preprocess element type FT and slice rank N and build `DataStructures.LRUCache{Dates.DateTime, Array{FT, N}}` (threading FT/N through the struct's CACHE type parameter). Then cache hits and get! returns are concretely typed, making read/read! type-stable and eliminating the per-read dynamic dispatch. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5b):** REAL, but impact modest. Confirmed L176 instantiates `LRUCache{Dates.DateTime, Array}` with the abstract `Array` (= `Array{T,N} where {T,N}`), so `read(fr, date)` infers an abstract `Array` return — a real return-type instability. However `read!`'s `dest .= read(...)` hits a `copyto!` function barrier, so the runtime cost is ≈1 dynamic dispatch per read, not per-element boxing. Effort is genuinely large (post-`preprocess_func` `FT`/`N` must be known at construction). Worth it for inference cleanliness; runtime win is small.
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:361`** _(impact: medium, effort: large)_ — **The non-interpolable else branch makes two full recursive calls into the same LinearPeriodFilling evaluate!, each of which re-derives all of the setup work (available_dates, available_periods, filters, _interpolable_range) from scratch.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate! (InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** Lines 361 and 364 call `evaluate!(dest, itp, date_pre, method)` and `evaluate!(tmp_field2, itp, date_post, method)` with the full period-filling method (not LinearInterpolation()). Each recursion re-enters at line 195 and repeats lines 208-266: unique_periods, the two filters, and _interpolable_range. So a single non-interpolable step pays roughly 3x the per-step setup cost. **Why hot:** Every timestep whose target date is outside the interpolable region (a primary use case for this method) triggers this branch, so the 3x amplification of the O(N)/allocating setup happens per step. date_pre/date_post are the interpolable-range boundaries moved to specific periods, so the recursion's setup largely recomputes invariants already available at the top level. **Fix:** Restructure so the invariant setup (available_dates, available_periods, and where possible dates_period_left/right and the interpolable range) is computed once and threaded into the recursion, or resolve date_pre/date_post to interpolable dates and dispatch them straight to the LinearInterpolation path instead of re-running the full method. Combined with hoisting available_periods (finding at line 211), this removes the repeated per-step rebuilds. **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5e): REAL but overstated.** The else branch does make two recursive `evaluate!(…, method)` calls (L361, L364), but `date_pre`/`date_post` are moved to real periods, so each recursion re-runs `unique_periods` (L211) and then hits the L218 interpolable-period early-return into `LinearInterpolation()` — it does NOT redo the filters / `_interpolable_range` as the summary claims. Net extra cost is ~2 redundant `unique_periods` (+ available_dates fetch) per non-interpolable step, i.e. a consequence of the already-flagged L211/Utils:389 recompute and fixed by the same precompute-at-construction change. Downgrade to low–medium; largely subsumed by that fix.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:255`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **`FT.(data_transformed)` unconditionally allocates a full grid-sized copy of the source data on every regrid, even when the data is already eltype FT and could be passed to the interpolant directly (or written into a reused buffer).** (in `Regridders.regrid`) **Mechanism:** `Intp.interpolate(dimensions_FT, FT.(data_transformed), ...)` broadcasts an element-type conversion over the entire source data array, allocating a fresh array each call. When `eltype(data_transformed) === FT` (common when the read data already matches the target undertype) this is a redundant full copy; when a conversion is genuinely needed it still allocates a new buffer every call instead of writing into a preallocated one. **Why hot:** Runs once per new time sample (per regrid cache-miss). The array is the full source data grid (e.g. 360x180x30 ~ 2M elements), so this is a grid-sized heap allocation + O(N) pass per snapshot, not a scalar cost. **Fix:** Skip the copy when types already match (`data_FT = eltype(data_transformed) === FT ? data_transformed : FT.(data_transformed)`), and/or preallocate a persistent FT work buffer sized to the data and convert in place (`copyto!`/broadcast-into) each call so the conversion reuses memory instead of allocating a new array per regrid. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5c):** REAL, moderate, medium. Confirmed `FT.(data_transformed)` (L255) broadcasts a grid-sized element-type conversion, allocating a fresh array on every regrid (per cache-miss / new time sample); redundant when `eltype(data_transformed) === FT`. The `eltype === FT ? data_transformed : FT.(...)` skip is safe. Caveat: `Interpolations.interpolate` may copy/retain the array internally, so the guaranteed saving is *our* copy in the type-matching case; the preallocated-buffer variant must ensure Interpolations does not alias the reused buffer.
- **`ext/NCFileReaderExt.jl:255`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **Every dated read rebuilds an invariant slicer as a heap-allocated Vector{Union{Colon,Int}}, recomputes dimnames(var), and then splats that runtime-length vector into getindex, causing a per-read allocation and dynamic-dispatch type instability.** (in `FileReaders.read(file_reader, date)`) **Mechanism:** L255-258 constructs `slicer = [i == time_index ? index : Colon() for i in 1:length(NCDatasets.dimnames(var))]`, a fresh `Vector{Union{Colon,Int64}}` allocated on every read, and calls `NCDatasets.dimnames(var)` again to get the rank. It is then splatted as `dataset[varname][slicer...]` (L259-260). Because the vector has a runtime length and a Union element type, the splat into `getindex` cannot be inferred, so the NetCDF slice indexing is a dynamic dispatch every call. The dimension structure (which axes are Colon, which axis is the time index, and the rank) is invariant across all reads for a given reader, so all of this is recomputed needlessly. **Why hot:** This is on the per-timestep dated-read path (once per read per step). It pays a small heap allocation (the slicer vector) plus a redundant dimnames lookup plus a type-unstable splat/dynamic-dispatch into getindex on every read; the type instability also poisons inference of the surrounding read return. **Fix:** Precompute the slicer template once at construction and store it in the struct (e.g. store the rank and a fixed tuple of Colon()/placeholder positions), then per read only update the time slot. For type stability build it as a tuple via `ntuple(i -> i == time_index ? index : Colon(), Val(N))` with N stored so the getindex splat is inferable, avoiding both the per-read Vector allocation and the dynamic dispatch. This is distinct from the L254 double-lookup finding (that one is about reusing the `var` binding). **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5e):** REAL, moderate, low–medium. Confirmed L255 builds `slicer = [i == time_index ? index : Colon() for i in 1:length(dimnames(var))]` — a fresh `Vector{Union{Colon,Int}}` per read — recomputes `dimnames(var)`, and splats it (runtime length) into `getindex`, so the NetCDF slice is a dynamic dispatch each read. The Vector is tiny (rank ~3–4), so the dominant cost is the type-unstable splat, not the allocation. A stored `ntuple(..., Val(N))` slicer template fixes both.
- **`ext/TempestRegridderExt.jl:214`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **No in-place regrid! variant: read_field materializes a brand-new grid-sized Field on every read, so even genuinely new dates (hot over time samples) pay a full grid-sized heap allocation per call.** (in `read_from_hdf5`) **Mechanism:** read_from_hdf5 calls ClimaCore.InputOutput.read_field (214), which allocates a fresh Field (grid-sized backing array) each call; swap_space (226) then wraps it. There is no path that reads into a caller-preallocated destination Field. Contrast InterpolationsRegridder, which offers Regridders.regrid! (InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:287) writing into an existing output field; TempestRegridder exposes only the allocating regrid. **Why hot:** On every new forcing date (the per-time-sample hot path, and every cache-miss through DataHandlingExt.jl:604), a new grid-sized array is allocated and discarded when the next date is read, generating GC pressure proportional to grid size (order Nelem*Nq^2*Nfields Float64) times the number of time samples processed over a run. **Fix:** Add a Regridders.regrid!(dest_field, regridder::TempestRegridder, date) that reads into a preallocated destination (e.g. via ClimaCore.InputOutput.read_field! / copying field_values into dest instead of allocating), and have callers reuse one destination Field across timesteps; keep the allocating regrid as a thin wrapper over it. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5e):** REAL, moderate, medium. Confirmed `read_from_hdf5` → `ClimaCore.InputOutput.read_field` (L214) allocates a fresh grid-sized Field per call, and there is no `regrid!` in-place variant (the sibling InterpolationsRegridder has one). Grid-sized heap allocation per new date. Complements the ⭐L114 no-memoization finding (distinct aspect: allocation vs disk re-read).
- **`ext/TimeVaryingInputs0DExt.jl:285`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **Per-timestep numeric-time evaluation of an ITime-backed 0D input rebuilds the ITime from scratch via the generic constructor plus promote, allocating an 8-element Vector and doing a linear period search + gcd/epoch reduction on every call, even though the target period and epoch are constant.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(destination, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput0D{<:AbstractArray{<:ITime}}, time::Number, args...; kwargs...)`) **Mechanism:** Line 285 does `converted_time = first(promote(ITime(time), itp.range[1]))`. `ITime(time)` for a Float64 dispatches to `src/ITime.jl:180` (and for an Int to `src/ITime.jl:56`), both of which allocate a fresh 8-element `Vector` of Dates period types (`periods`/`potential_periods`) on the heap and iterate over it computing `Dates.tons`/divisions/`isinteger` until a representable period is found. Then `promote` (src/ITime.jl:237) runs `find_common_epoch` (generator + reduce over `_unique_epochs`) and `reduce(gcd, ...)` and a `map`. All of this recomputes, every step, a conversion whose period and epoch are already fixed by `itp.range[1]` for the entire simulation. **Why hot:** This is the evaluate! entry point taken on every simulation timestep whenever a 0D input built with ITime `times` is driven with a plain numeric time (a supported/common coupling pattern; the overload exists solely for it). Cost per step is one heap allocation of an 8-element Vector plus an up-to-8-iteration linear scan plus a promote (gcd + epoch reduction) — strictly more expensive than the already-catalogued 1-element-Vector allocation at L76, though still scalar-sized (GC pressure, not grid-sized). By contrast the sibling DateTime overload (L266-267) already avoids this by constructing the ITime with an explicit period/epoch (the cheap 3-arg path), and the numeric->Number overload at L251 is a plain scalar conversion. **Fix:** Stop calling the generic `ITime(time)` + `promote` on the hot path. Since `r0 = itp.range[1]` has a fixed `period(r0)` and `epoch(r0)`, construct the converted time directly via the cheap 3-arg constructor `ITime(counter, period, epoch)`, mirroring the DateTime overload: compute the counter from `time` (seconds) using the known period once (e.g. cache the seconds-per-period / conversion factor in the struct at construction, or compute `round(typeof(counter(r0)), time / float(period-in-seconds))`), then build `ITime(counter, period(r0), epoch(r0))`. This removes the per-step 8-element Vector allocation, the linear period search, and the promote. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5c):** REAL, effort moderate; impact low–medium (scalar). Confirmed `ITime(time)` for a Float hits ITime.jl:180, which allocates the 8-element `periods` Vector and linear-scans it (`isinteger` test per period); the Int path (ITime.jl:56) likewise allocates `potential_periods`; then `promote(...)` adds `find_common_epoch` + `gcd`. All per timestep even though `itp.range[1]`'s `period`/`epoch` are fixed. Building via the 3-arg positional constructor removes it. Allocation is scalar-sized (GC pressure), not grid-sized.
- **`ext/TimeVaryingInputsExt.jl:392`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **Slicing the preallocated-fields Tuple with a runtime UnitRange (`[begin:(begin+1)]`) is type-unstable, forcing `field_t0`/`field_t1` to infer as Any and de-specializing the grid-sized broadcasts.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, time, ::LinearInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `itp.preallocated_regridded_fields` is a Tuple (ntuple, line 143). Indexing a Tuple with a UnitRange value (`begin:(begin+1)`) returns a Tuple whose length is not encoded in the range type, so inference cannot determine the element type and returns an abstract `Tuple`; the destructured `field_t0`, `field_t1` become `::Any`. The subsequent fused broadcasts `dest .= (1 - coeff) .* field_t0 .+ coeff .* field_t1` (lines 409, 426) then cannot be specialized on the concrete Field type, incurring dynamic dispatch (and risking a materialized temporary) over grid-sized data. The identical anti-pattern appears at ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:240 (`[(end - 1):end]`), feeding the broadcasts at LPF lines 290/298. **Why hot:** This is on the DEFAULT LinearInterpolation path, executed every timestep, and the affected broadcast is grid-sized (O(10^4-10^6) points). Type instability at this point de-optimizes the single most-used interpolation kernel. **Fix:** Index the tuple elements individually and stably: `field_t0, field_t1 = itp.preallocated_regridded_fields[begin], itp.preallocated_regridded_fields[begin + 1]` (and `[end - 1], [end]` at LPF:240), or use `ntuple`/`Base.front` with a `Val` length. Verify with `@code_warntype` that field_t0/field_t1 infer as the concrete Field type. **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5b): PARTIAL — fix valid, impact overstated.** The tuple-slice-by-range gotcha is real (`t[begin:(begin+1)]` yields a runtime-length result), and the scalar-index fix is unambiguously type-stable + allocation-free, so **apply it**. BUT `preallocated_regridded_fields` is a *homogeneous* `NTuple{2,F}` (`ntuple(_ -> zeros(target_space), 2)`, L142), so inference most likely recovers `Tuple{Vararg{F}}` and the destructured `field_t0/field_t1` stay concrete `F` — meaning the grid-sized broadcasts are probably NOT de-specialized to `Any`. Downgrade impact to **low**; the only sure cost is the type-unstable / possibly-allocating intermediate tuple. Confirming the broadcast-specialization claim needs `@code_warntype` (not run here, per no-Julia constraint). Same downgrade applies to the sibling `[(end-1):end]` at linearperiodfilling.jl:240.
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:211`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **LinearPeriodFilling recomputes the invariant `available_periods` set from scratch on every evaluate! call.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, time, ::LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `available_periods = unique_periods(available_dates, period)` runs `map(d -> beginningofperiod(d, period), dates) |> unique |> sort` (Utils.jl:389): an O(N) allocating map, an O(N) `unique` (builds a Set + Vector), and an O(N log N) `sort` allocating another Vector. The result depends only on `data_handler.available_dates` and `method.period`, both fixed for the lifetime of the input, yet it is rebuilt every timestep. It is also used immediately in the O(N) membership test `_extract_period(target_date, period) in available_periods` on line 218. **Why hot:** Runs once per LinearPeriodFilling evaluate! (i.e. per simulation timestep for seasonal-cycle filling inputs). available_dates for climate forcing is commonly hundreds to tens of thousands of entries, so every step pays 2-3 fresh heap allocations plus an O(N log N) sort to reconstruct a constant. **Fix:** Compute `available_periods` (and, ideally, the per-period date subsets) once at construction when `method isa LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation` and store it in the InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D struct (add a field), or memoize it keyed on the data handler. Read the cached value in evaluate! instead of calling unique_periods per step.
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:247`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **Two `filter` calls allocate fresh date vectors and scan all available_dates every step, and recompute `endofperiod` once per element inside the closure.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, time, ::LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `dates_period_left = filter(d -> period_left <= d <= endofperiod(period_left, period), available_dates)` (line 247) and the analogous `dates_period_right` (line 253) each allocate a new Vector and walk the entire sorted available_dates O(N). Worse, `endofperiod(period_left, period)` (and `endofperiod(period_right, period)`) is inside the filter predicate, so it is recomputed for every element instead of once, adding N redundant date-arithmetic calls per filter. Because available_dates is sorted, each per-period subset is a contiguous slice obtainable by binary search. **Why hot:** Reached on every LinearPeriodFilling evaluate! whenever the target date is not within an available period (the normal case for cross-period seasonal filling), i.e. per timestep; two O(N) allocations plus 2N loop-invariant endofperiod calls per step over a dates vector that may be thousands long. **Fix:** Hoist `ep_left = endofperiod(period_left, period)` out of the closure, then use binary search on the sorted array and a non-allocating view: `lo = searchsortedfirst(available_dates, period_left); hi = searchsortedlast(available_dates, ep_left); dates_period_left = @view available_dates[lo:hi]` (same for right). This is O(log N) and allocation-free. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5d):** REAL, moderate, medium. Confirmed both `filter(...)` calls (L247/L253) allocate a fresh Vector and scan all of sorted `available_dates` O(N), and `endofperiod(period_left/right, period)` sits inside the predicate so it is recomputed per element (N redundant calls). Reached per non-interpolable step. The predicate is a contiguous range on sorted data, so `searchsortedfirst`/`searchsortedlast` + `@view` (with `endofperiod` hoisted) is an equivalent O(log N), allocation-free replacement. N = date-axis length, so meaningful for long axes.
- **`src/Utils.jl:389`** _(impact: medium, effort: moderate)_ — **unique_periods rebuilds an O(N log N), triple-allocating unique+sort of period-starts on every LinearPeriodFilling evaluate!, over data that is already sorted and invariant for the input's lifetime.** (in `unique_periods`) **Mechanism:** `map(d -> beginningofperiod(d, period), dates) |> unique |> sort` allocates a fresh Vector of N DateTimes from `map`, then `unique` builds a hash Set plus a second Vector, then `sort` does O(N log N) work (and may allocate a third buffer). Because `dates` here is `available_dates` (kept sorted; the rest of DataHandlingExt.jl relies on that via searchsortedfirst) and `beginningofperiod` is monotonic non-decreasing, the mapped array is already sorted — so the hashing inside `unique` and the entire `sort` are pure redundancy. A single linear pass that keeps consecutive-distinct values produces the identical result in O(N) with one allocation. **Why hot:** Invoked unconditionally at the very top of TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, ..., ::LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation) (ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:211), i.e. once per simulation timestep for LinearPeriodFilling inputs, and before the early-return branch at line 218. N = number of available time samples (~480 for 40 yrs monthly, ~14600 for daily), so each timestep pays O(N log N) plus 3 heap allocations sized ~N, while the useful output is only P unique periods (tiny, e.g. tens). The result depends solely on (available_dates, period), both fixed for the input's lifetime, so it is entirely recomputed each step. **Fix:** Two complementary changes. (a) Rewrite unique_periods to exploit the sorted, monotonic input: iterate `dates` once, pushing `beginningofperiod(d, period)` only when it differs from the last pushed value, dropping both `unique` (Set) and `sort` — O(N), single allocation. (b) Structurally better: since (available_dates, period) are invariant, compute `available_periods` once at construction (store it as a field on the InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D or the LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation method) and read it in evaluate! instead of calling unique_periods every timestep. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5b):** REAL, medium. Confirmed `unique_periods` = `map(beginningofperiod) |> unique |> sort` (3 allocations + O(N log N), Utils.jl:389) and that it runs unconditionally at linearperiodfilling.jl:211 **before** the early-return at :218 — i.e. every LinearPeriodFilling timestep. `beginningofperiod` is monotonic non-decreasing (year/month/day/week floors) and `available_dates` is sorted, so the mapped array is already sorted → `unique`'s Set and the `sort` are pure redundancy; the O(N) single-pass rewrite is correct, and precomputing `available_periods` once at construction is better still. (Same root issue as the linearperiodfilling.jl:211 entry above.)
- **`src/DataStructures.jl:277`** _(impact: low, effort: large)_ — **The LRU recency queue is a plain Vector{K}, so every cache access does an O(N) linear scan (filter!) to move the accessed key to the back, where a proper LRU is O(1).** (in `_update_priority!`) **Mechanism:** _update_priority! runs `filter!(k -> k != key, cache.priority)` — a full linear traversal of the priority vector with no early exit — to delete the key before re-pushing it at the end. This move-to-back is O(N) in the number of cached entries. It is the single shared recency-bookkeeping routine invoked by every mutating/reading accessor (setindex! L49, getindex L62, both get! forms L74/L93), so the O(N) cost is paid on the common cache-HIT path too, even though nothing is inserted. The design keeps two structures (the Dict and the priority Vector) in sync and reconciles them with an O(N) scan on each touch. **Why hot:** Both production caches route through here per step. The FileReaders read cache (LRUCache{DateTime,Array}, default max_size=128, NCFileReaderExt.jl:176) is touched via getindex on every cache-hit read (NCFileReaderExt.jl:237) and via do-block get! on every miss (L242/L283). The DataHandler regrid cache (LRUCache{DateTime,Field}, max_size configurable, DataHandlingExt.jl:366) is touched via get! on every regridded_snapshot (DataHandlingExt.jl:604). So per read / per regrid it scans up to max_size entries (128 for reads) instead of O(1). Absolute cost at defaults is small (scalar DateTime comparisons, and it is dwarfed by the disk read on a miss and the grid-sized array copy on a hit), but it is O(N) rather than O(1) and grows linearly if max_size is raised — the struct's own docstring (L27-30) already flags that 'another data structure could be more efficient here.' **Fix:** Replace the Vector{K} + filter!/push! recency scheme with an O(1) structure. Preferred: back the cache with OrderedCollections.OrderedDict{K,V} and call Base.move_to_end!/delete+reinsert on access; or keep a Dict{K,Node} plus a doubly-linked list so move-to-back and evict-front are both O(1). If the Vector must be kept, at least give the scan an early exit: `idx = findfirst(isequal(key), cache.priority); idx !== nothing && deleteat!(cache.priority, idx)` (still O(N), only a partial mitigation). Note the correctness fix already filed for this line — comparisons must use isequal, not != — should be preserved in whatever replacement is chosen. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5b):** REAL but LOW / marginal. Confirmed `_update_priority!` runs `filter!(k -> k != key, priority)` + `push!` on every access (getindex L62 and all `get!` forms L74/L93), and the `key`-capturing closure adds ~1 small alloc per access. BUT N = current entries ≤ `max_size` (2 for DataHandler, 128 for NCFileReader), so the "O(N)" is O(small const) and is dwarfed by the disk read (miss) or grid-sized copy (hit). The O(1) linked-list/OrderedDict refactor is **not** justified at current defaults; a cheap early-exit (`idx = findfirst(isequal(key), priority); idx !== nothing && deleteat!(priority, idx)`) is the pragmatic take. Shares this line with the Pass-4 `!=`-vs-`isequal` correctness bug — fix both together.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:210`** _(impact: low, effort: moderate)_ — **`dimensions_FT` re-converts (and possibly re-reverses) the dimension axes to FT on every regrid, though the axes are the same fixed object each call.** (in `Regridders.regrid`) **Mechanism:** `map(dimensions, regridder.dim_increasing) do dim, increasing; !increasing ? reverse(FT.(dim)) : FT.(dim) end` allocates a fresh converted array for every dimension on every call. `data_handler.dimensions` (DataHandlingExt.jl:306,630) is fixed at construction and passed unchanged to every regrid, so the FT-converted axes are invariant yet recomputed and re-allocated per snapshot. **Why hot:** Per new-time-sample regrid. Allocates one array per spatial dimension sized to each axis length (e.g. lon 360 + lat 180 + z 30), plus the extra reverse copy for decreasing dims, on every call — repeated O(sum of dim lengths) allocation of a constant. **Fix:** Precompute the FT-converted (and reversed, and lon-augmented) dimension axes once — either cache them on the regridder keyed by the dimensions object, or have DataHandler store/pass already-FT axes — and at minimum short-circuit `FT.(dim)` when `eltype(dim) === FT` and the dimension is increasing so no new array is allocated. **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5d): REAL but low (axis-sized, not grid-sized).** Confirmed `dimensions_FT` (L210) re-`FT.()`s (and reverses) each dimension axis every regrid over the invariant `data_handler.dimensions`. But these are the 1-D coordinate axes (lengths like lon 360 + lat 180 + z 30), so the per-call allocation is O(sum of axis lengths), NOT grid-sized (O(product)). The precompute-once / `eltype===FT` short-circuit is clean, but the absolute saving is small next to the grid-sized copies elsewhere in `regrid`.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:245`** _(impact: low, effort: moderate)_ — **On the periodic-longitude 'oncell' path, `_append_first_slice` (a `cat`) makes a second full grid-sized copy of the data in addition to the `FT.()` copy, and the oncell decision itself (isequispaced + 360-span scan) is recomputed every regrid despite being invariant.** (in `Regridders.regrid`) **Mechanism:** When `lon_oncell` is true, `data_transformed = _append_first_slice(data_transformed, lon_dim_idx)` uses `cat(data, view(...), dims=dim_idx)` to allocate an augmented copy (grid size + one lon slice), and then line 255 `FT.(data_transformed)` copies the whole thing AGAIN — two grid-sized allocations per call. Additionally, the `lon_oncell` computation at lines 226-241 runs `isequispaced(dim)` (an O(dim) scan) plus the span check every call, even though the longitude axis (and therefore the decision) is identical across all calls. **Why hot:** Both run per new-time-sample regrid whenever a periodic 360-degree equispaced longitude axis is used (a common CF-convention layout). The `cat` allocation is grid-sized and is immediately followed by another grid-sized `FT.()` allocation, doubling the source-data memory traffic; the equispaced scan is O(number of longitudes) repeated every snapshot. **Fix:** Cache the `lon_oncell` decision (and the augmented dimension axis) once since the longitude axis is invariant, and fuse the slice-append with the FT conversion so only one grid-sized buffer is produced — e.g. build the FT-typed augmented array in a single pass (or write into a preallocated buffer of the augmented size) instead of `cat` followed by a separate `FT.()` copy. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5e):** REAL, moderate, medium (periodic-360 lon only). Confirmed `lon_oncell` (L226-241, incl. an `isequispaced(dim)` O(len) scan) is recomputed every regrid though the longitude axis is invariant, and `_append_first_slice` (L275) does `cat(data, view, dims)` — an augmented grid-sized copy — which the `FT.()` at L255 then copies AGAIN (two grid-sized allocations). Cache the oncell decision + augmented axis once and fuse the append with the FT conversion. Builds on the :255 (`FT.()`) and :276 (`[1]`) findings.
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:172`** _(impact: low, effort: moderate)_ — **dates_period_right_moved_to_left materializes a whole shifted array via broadcast every call, but is only ever reduced to its min and max.** (in `_interpolable_range`) **Mechanism:** Line 172 `dates_period_right_moved_to_left = dates_period_right .- period_offset` allocates a new array the size of one period's dates, and the only subsequent uses are minimum(...) (line 185) and maximum(...) (line 189). The shift can be pushed into the scalar reductions instead of allocating the intermediate array. **Why hot:** _interpolable_range runs once per timestep (line 266) and again in the else-branch recursion; the broadcast allocates an O(N_period) DateTime array each time solely to take two scalars. **Fix:** Drop the intermediate array and compute `min_right = minimum(dates_period_right) - period_offset` and `max_right = maximum(dates_period_right) - period_offset`, then combine with scalar `max`/`min`. (This also composes with the already-catalogued 183/187 literal-array-reduction fix.) **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5e):** REAL, low. Confirmed `dates_period_right_moved_to_left = dates_period_right .- period_offset` (L172) allocates a period-sized Vector used only for `minimum`/`maximum` (L185/189); subtracting a constant offset commutes with min/max, so `minimum(dates_period_right) - period_offset` / `maximum(...)` removes the array. Period-sized (small), not grid-sized — low impact; composes with the :183 literal-array-reduction fix.

### Easy wins (short, low-risk — roughly ≤ 3 lines each)

- **`ext/DataHandlingExt.jl:604`** _(impact: medium)_ — **The get! do-block closure is constructed on every call, including cache hits, causing a heap allocation per regridded_snapshot.** (in `DataHandling.regridded_snapshot(data_handler::DataHandler, date::Dates.DateTime)`) **Mechanism:** `get!(data_handler._cached_regridded_fields, date) do ... end` builds an anonymous closure capturing `data_handler`, `varnames`, `compose_function`, `regridder_type`, and `date`. That closure object is materialized and passed into `Base.get!` (DataStructures.jl:88, which forwards to Dict `get!`) before the cache is consulted, so it is allocated even when the key is already present and the body never runs. It flows through two non-inlined generic calls, so escape analysis cannot elide it. **Fix:** Add a fast path before the get! so cache hits never build the closure: `cache = data_handler._cached_regridded_fields; haskey(cache, date) && return cache[date]` and then `return get!(cache, date) do ... end`. `cache[date]` (getindex) still bumps LRU recency, so semantics are preserved and the closure is only allocated on a real miss. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5b):** REAL, medium. Confirmed `regridded_snapshot` is the per-timestep entry (L588) and the `do`-block closure captures 5 locals and is passed across the non-inlined `LRUCache` `get!` (DataStructures.jl:88), so it heap-allocates on every call including cache hits. The fast-path fix is correct — `cache[date]` getindex calls `_update_priority!` (DataStructures.jl:62), preserving LRU recency. Allocation is scalar-sized (GC pressure per step), not grid-sized.
- **`ext/NCFileReaderExt.jl:259`** _(impact: medium)_ — **With the default preprocess_func = identity, the hot dated read does identity.(slice), copying the entire freshly-read grid slice every read.** (in `FileReaders.read(file_reader::NCFileReader, date::Dates.DateTime)`) **Mechanism:** var[slicer...] already returns a brand-new, unaliased Array from NCDatasets; broadcasting identity over it (`preprocess_func.(...)`) materializes a second full-size array (a pure memcpy of the whole grid) that is discarded work when preprocess_func is the default identity. **Fix:** Return the fresh slice directly when no real preprocessing is needed: data = var[slicer...] return file_reader.preprocess_func === identity ? data : file_reader.preprocess_func.(data) (reuses `var` per the already-noted L254 fix; safe because the slice is freshly allocated and unaliased). **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5c):** REAL, easy, medium. Confirmed default `preprocess_func = identity` (L92) and that `dataset[varname][slicer...]` materializes a fresh, unaliased `Array` from NCDatasets, so `identity.(...)` allocates a second full grid-sized copy that is immediately discarded — a wasted memcpy on every dated read (per timestep). The `preprocess_func === identity ? data : preprocess_func.(data)` guard is correct and ownership-safe.
- **`ext/TimeVaryingInputsExt.jl:416`** _(impact: medium)_ — **The in-range guard `time in DataHandling.available_dates(itp.data_handler)` is an O(N) linear membership scan over the sorted available_dates vector on every LinearInterpolation timestep.** (in `evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, time, ::LinearInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `available_dates(data_handler)` (ext/DataHandlingExt.jl:418) returns the stored sorted `Vector{DateTime}` unchanged, so `time in vec` (`∈` on an AbstractVector) walks the entire array O(N) per call, even though the array is sorted and an O(log N) binary search suffices. **Fix:** Replace the linear `in` with a binary-search membership test on the sorted vector: `dates = DataHandling.available_dates(itp.data_handler); idx = searchsortedfirst(dates, time); if idx <= lastindex(dates) && dates[idx] == time` (keeping the existing `regridded_snapshot!(dest, itp.data_handler, time)` in the then-branch and the previous_date/next_date interpolation in the else-branch). `searchsortedfirst` is Base and already used elsewhere in the package. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5b):** REAL, easy. Confirmed `available_dates` returns the stored `Vector{DateTime}` unchanged (DataHandlingExt.jl:418), kept sorted (previous_date/next_date use `searchsortedfirst` on it), so `time in vec` is an O(N) scan on the **default** LinearInterpolation path (L416), run every timestep. The `searchsortedfirst` rewrite is O(log N) and behavior-preserving. Per-comparison cost is small, so impact scales with the date-axis length (meaningful for long climate axes, minor for short ones).
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:240`** _(impact: medium)_ — **Range-indexing the `preallocated_regridded_fields` Tuple with `[(end-1):end]` is type-unstable and allocates.** (in `evaluate!(::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, ::LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `preallocated_regridded_fields` is a `Tuple` (built via `ntuple` in TimeVaryingInputsExt.jl:143). Indexing a tuple with a runtime `UnitRange` (`(end-1):end`) hits `getindex(::Tuple, ::AbstractUnitRange)`, whose result length is not a compile-time constant, so it infers as an abstract `Tuple` and heap-allocates a new tuple every call, then destructures it via dynamic dispatch. **Fix:** Replace with two scalar indexes (both compile-time-constant, type-stable, no allocation): ```julia tmp_field1 = itp.preallocated_regridded_fields[end - 1] tmp_field2 = itp.preallocated_regridded_fields[end] ```
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:297`** _(impact: medium)_ — **Two separate in-place broadcasts (`tmp_field1 .*= coeff; dest .+= tmp_field1`) can be fused into one.** (in `evaluate!(::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, ::LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `tmp_field1 .*= coeff` followed by `dest .+= tmp_field1` launches two broadcast kernels and needlessly mutates the temp field. They can be fused into a single kernel `@. dest += tmp_field1 * coeff`, halving the kernel launches over the field (relevant on GPU/ClimaCore fields). The identical pattern also occurs at lines 365-366 (`tmp_field2 .*= coeff; dest .+= tmp_field2`). **Fix:** Lines 297-298 -> `@. dest += tmp_field1 * coeff` ; Lines 365-366 -> `@. dest += tmp_field2 * coeff`. (Also drops the now-unnecessary mutation of the temp field.) **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5d):** REAL, easy, medium. Confirmed the two-kernel pattern at L297-298 (`tmp_field1 .*= coeff; dest .+= tmp_field1`) and again at L365-366; `tmp_field1`/`tmp_field2` are not read afterward (each block `return nothing`s), so `@. dest += tmp_field1 * coeff` is semantically identical and halves the grid-sized broadcast kernels — a real win on GPU/ClimaCore fields. `dest` already holds the `(1-coeff)` term, so correctness is preserved.
- **`ext/DataHandlingExt.jl:455`** _(impact: low)_ — **round returns a Float that is then converted to Int64 inside the Millisecond constructor, an avoidable Float->Int round-trip.** (in `DataHandling.time_to_date(data_handler::DataHandler, time::AbstractFloat)`) **Mechanism:** `Dates.Millisecond(round(1_000 * time))`: `round(1_000*time)` yields a Float64, which the `Millisecond` constructor then converts to its `Int64` field, doing the float->int conversion a second time. `round(Int64, x)` produces the Int64 directly (same RoundNearest mode), skipping the extra conversion and keeping the value integer-typed. **Fix:** Use `time_ms = Dates.Millisecond(round(Int64, 1_000 * time))`. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL but negligible.** `round(1_000*time)` returns a Float that `Dates.Millisecond` then converts to Int64; `round(Int64, …)` does it directly, saving one scalar float→int conversion. Correct, but a single-instruction win — apply for tidiness, not speed.
- **`ext/DataHandlingExt.jl:596`** _(impact: low)_ — **file_paths is eagerly computed via a string-keyed Dict lookup on every call, but is only used to format an error that almost never fires.** (in `DataHandling.regridded_snapshot(data_handler::DataHandler, date::Dates.DateTime)`) **Mechanism:** `file_paths = data_handler.file_readers[first(varnames)].file_paths` runs a `Dict{String,...}` lookup (hashing the variable-name string) plus a field access unconditionally on every non-static call, purely so the value is available for the `error(...)` string on the next line. On the common in-range path the error is never raised, so the lookup and the string it prepares are wasted. **Fix:** Drop the local and inline the lookup into the error so it is only evaluated when the guard actually fails: `date in data_handler.available_dates || error("Date $date not available in files $(data_handler.file_readers[first(varnames)].file_paths)")`. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low.** Confirmed `file_paths = data_handler.file_readers[first(varnames)].file_paths` (L596) is a string-keyed `Dict` lookup done on every non-static call purely to build an error that fires only when `date ∉ available_dates`; inlining it into the `||` error makes it lazy. Correct but small (one hash lookup/step).
- **`ext/DataHandlingExt.jl:601`** _(impact: low)_ — **regridder_type is computed before the get! but is only referenced inside the do-block, so it is dead work on the cache-hit path.** (in `DataHandling.regridded_snapshot(data_handler::DataHandler, date::Dates.DateTime)`) **Mechanism:** `regridder_type = nameof(typeof(data_handler.regridder))` is used only at lines 605 and 613 inside the get! closure (the cache-miss branch). Because it is computed in the outer scope it is evaluated on every call, including cache hits where the closure body never executes. (If the compiler constant-folds `nameof(typeof(...))` the change is a harmless no-op; if it does not, moving it removes a per-hit computation.) **Fix:** Move the assignment to the first line inside the `get!(...) do ... end` block (just before `if regridder_type == :TempestRegridder`), so it is only computed on an actual cache miss. **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5c): REAL but marginal.** `regridder_type = nameof(typeof(data_handler.regridder))` (L601) is indeed referenced only inside the do-block (L605/613), so it's outer-scope dead work on cache hits. BUT the regridder type is concrete, so `nameof(typeof(...))` is very likely constant-folded → the runtime win is probably zero. Worth moving inside the do-block for cleanliness (bundle with the L604 closure fix); do not expect a measurable speedup.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:183`** _(impact: low)_ — **regrid's Z-only-with-3D-data branch allocates a Vector{Int} via [i for i in 1:3 if i != z_idx] just to hold the two horizontal dimension indices.** (in `Regridders.regrid`) **Mechanism:** The array comprehension heap-allocates a Vector each call (then it is only indexed at [1] and [2]); a non-allocating tuple generator yields the same two indices without the heap array. **Fix:** horiz_indices = Tuple(i for i in 1:3 if i != z_idx) **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL (2-elem Vector alloc) but the suggested fix is flawed.** `Tuple(i for … if i != z_idx)` builds a Tuple from a *filtered* generator whose length the compiler cannot know → itself type-unstable (and may not avoid allocation). Since `z_idx ∈ {1,2,3}`, a type-stable, non-allocating fix is `horiz_indices = ntuple(i -> i < z_idx ? i : i + 1, 2)`. Only on the Z-only-3D branch; impact low.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:218`** _(impact: low)_ — **regrid builds a throwaway Vector{Int} comprehension and then wraps it with Tuple(...) just to collect the decreasing-dimension indices.** (in `Regridders.regrid`) **Mechanism:** The array comprehension [i for (i, d) in enumerate(...) if !d] heap-allocates a Vector each call before Tuple(...) converts it. Feeding a lazy generator straight into Tuple removes the intermediate Vector allocation. **Fix:** decreasing_indices = Tuple(i for (i, d) in enumerate(regridder.dim_increasing) if !d) **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low.** The `[…]`→`Tuple(…)` does allocate a throwaway Vector, but `regridder.dim_increasing` is fixed at construction, so `decreasing_indices` is **invariant** — precomputing it once at construction beats both the Vector and the suggested `Tuple(generator)` (which, from a filtered generator, is itself length-unstable). Only runs when a dim decreases; impact low.
- **`ext/InterpolationsRegridderExt.jl:276`** _(impact: low)_ — **_append_first_slice allocates a 1-element Vector{Int} ([1]) to select the first slice instead of using an isbits 1:1 range.** (in `_append_first_slice`) **Mechanism:** [1] heap-allocates a Vector every call when constructing the indexing tuple; 1:1 is an isbits UnitRange that produces the identical keep-dimension view with no allocation. **Fix:** slice_indices = ntuple(i -> i == dim_idx ? (1:1) : Colon(), ndims(data)) **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low.** Confirmed `_append_first_slice` (L275-278) builds its index tuple with `[1]` (a heap-allocated 1-element `Vector`); `1:1` is an isbits `UnitRange` that keeps the dimension identically with no allocation. Per-regrid on the periodic-lon path; tiny alloc, but a clean one-token fix.
- **`ext/TempestRegridderExt.jl:218`** _(impact: low)_ — **The per-read validation block recomputes axes(field) three separate times instead of binding it once and reusing it.** (in `read_from_hdf5`) **Mechanism:** axes(field) is evaluated in each of the three @assert lines (L218, L220, L222) to fetch the field's space, so the same lookup is performed 3x per read when a single binding would suffice. **Fix:** Bind once before the asserts and reuse: `field_axes = axes(field)` then compare `ClimaCore.Spaces.topology(field_axes) == ClimaCore.Spaces.topology(space)`, `ClimaCore.Spaces.quadrature_style(field_axes) == ...`, and `ClimaCore.Spaces.global_geometry(field_axes) == ...`. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL but negligible.** Confirmed `axes(field)` is called in all three `@assert`s (L218/220/222); binding `field_axes = axes(field)` once removes two redundant calls. But `axes(field)` is a cheap accessor (returns the stored space, no alloc), so 3×→1× is trivial — an easy tidy-up, not a speedup.
- **`ext/TimeVaryingInputs0DExt.jl:182`** _(impact: low)_ — **The half-step midpoint test `time >= t_end + 0.5dt` uses float-times-ITime multiplication, which invokes a guarded round/convert helper on every periodic nearest-neighbor evaluate for ITime inputs.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput0D, time, ::NearestNeighbor)`) **Mechanism:** For ITime-based inputs `dt` is an `ITime`, so `0.5dt` dispatches to `Base.:*(a::AbstractFloat, t::ITime)` (src/ITime.jl:303), which does a bounds-guard branch (`a>1||a<0`), a float multiply, a `round(typeof(counter), ...)`, and an `ITime` reconstruction, then `t_end + 0.5dt` does another ITime add. All of this is avoidable: the inequality can be evaluated with pure integer ITime arithmetic. For Float inputs the rewrite is cost-neutral. **Fix:** Replace L182 `time >= t_end + 0.5dt && (time = t_init)` with the algebraically identical integer form `2 * (time - t_end) >= dt && (time = t_init)`, which keeps ITime arithmetic in the integer counter type and avoids the guarded float-multiply/round in Base.:*(::AbstractFloat, ::ITime). **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low (ITime periodic-NN only).** Confirmed L182 `time >= t_end + 0.5dt` with an `ITime` `dt` dispatches `0.5dt` to `Base.:*(::AbstractFloat, ::ITime)` (ITime.jl:303) — a guarded round/convert + ITime rebuild — per periodic nearest-neighbor evaluate. The integer form `2*(time - t_end) >= dt` is algebraically identical and stays in integer counter arithmetic. Only bites ITime-backed 0D inputs; scalar cost.
- **`ext/TimeVaryingInputs0DExt.jl:221`** _(impact: low)_ — **In the PeriodicCalendar past-t_end extrapolation branch, `itp.vals[end]` is indexed twice (and `itp.vals[begin]` once) in each of the two broadcast expressions, repeating the same array getindex instead of computing the endpoints once.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput0D, time, ::LinearInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** Both branches (L220-224 and L226-228) contain `itp.vals[end]` twice, so the boundary values are re-fetched via getindex on each occurrence. `itp.vals` is an arbitrary `AbstractArray` whose getindex the compiler cannot always prove pure/CSE-able, so this can be a redundant scalar index (extra bounds check + load) per call; hoisting `vals_end`/`vals_begin` into locals guarantees a single fetch. **Fix:** Right after `if time > t_end` (L217) add `vals_end = itp.vals[end]` and `vals_begin = itp.vals[begin]`, then use `vals_end`/`vals_begin` in both @.-broadcasts, e.g. `@. dest = vals_end + FT((vals_begin - vals_end) / float(dt) * float((time - t_end)))` and `@. dest = vals_end + (vals_begin - vals_end) / dt * (time - t_end)`. **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low.** Confirmed both past-t_end branches (L220-224, L226-228) index `itp.vals[end]` twice (and `itp.vals[begin]` once) per broadcast; `itp.vals` is an arbitrary `AbstractArray`, so hoisting `vals_end`/`vals_begin` guarantees a single fetch. Scalar getindex — minor, but a safe hoist.
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:68`** _(impact: low)_ — **`findfirst(d -> d >= beginningofperiod(date, period), available_periods)` is an O(N) predicate scan of the sorted available_periods vector where a single searchsortedfirst (O(log N)) computes the same index.** (in `_neighboring_periods`) **Mechanism:** available_periods is sorted (from unique_periods, Utils.jl:389). `findfirst(d -> d >= x, sorted_vec)` returns the first index whose value is >= x, which is exactly `searchsortedfirst(sorted_vec, x)`. The current form is O(N) and additionally recomputes `beginningofperiod(date, period)` for every element visited instead of once. **Fix:** Hoist the bound and use searchsortedfirst: `target = beginningofperiod(date, period); index_period_right = searchsortedfirst(available_periods, target)` (O(log N), and beginningofperiod is evaluated once). **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5c): REAL but low.** Confirmed `_neighboring_periods` (called per non-interpolable step at L244) uses `findfirst(d -> d >= beginningofperiod(date,period), available_periods)` on the sorted `available_periods`; `searchsortedfirst(available_periods, target)` replaces it in O(log N) and hoists `beginningofperiod` out of the per-element closure — correct. BUT N here = number of *unique periods* (tens, e.g. ~40 for 40 yrs), not the full date axis, so the O(N)→O(log N) absolute win is small. Clean but marginal.
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:159`** _(impact: low)_ — **The two `length(unique(beginningofperiod.(...))) != 1` validation checks each allocate a broadcast array plus a `unique` array.** (in `_interpolable_range`) **Mechanism:** `beginningofperiod.(dates_period_left, period)` allocates a full Vector, `unique(...)` allocates another, and only its length is inspected. Same at line 162 for `dates_period_right`. Four throwaway arrays per call just to check that all dates map to one period; `allequal` over a lazy generator does this with zero allocation. **Fix:** ```julia if !allequal(beginningofperiod(d, period) for d in dates_period_left) error("dates in dates_period_left belongs to different periods") end if !allequal(beginningofperiod(d, period) for d in dates_period_right) error("dates in dates_period_right belongs to different periods") end ``` (`allequal` is available on the declared Julia 1.10 compat.)
- **`ext/time_varying_inputs_linearperiodfilling.jl:218`** _(impact: low)_ — **`_extract_period(target_date, period) in available_periods` performs an O(N) linear membership scan of the sorted available_periods vector on every LinearPeriodFilling evaluate! call.** (in `TimeVaryingInputs.evaluate!(dest, itp::InterpolatingTimeVaryingInput23D, time, method::LinearPeriodFillingInterpolation)`) **Mechanism:** `available_periods` is produced by `unique_periods(...)` which ends in `|> unique |> sort` (Utils.jl:389), so it is sorted. `in` on this Vector is a linear scan. Because the vector is sorted, `insorted(_extract_period(target_date, period), available_periods)` answers the same question in O(log N). **Fix:** Change `if _extract_period(target_date, period) in available_periods` to `if insorted(_extract_period(target_date, period), available_periods)` (available_periods is already sorted). **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low.** Confirmed L218 `_extract_period(target_date, period) in available_periods` is a linear scan of the sorted `available_periods`; `insorted(...)` is O(log N) and equivalent. BUT N = #unique periods (tens), so the absolute win is small — clean, low impact.
- **`src/ITime.jl:335`** _(impact: low)_ — **The ITime range generator constructs each element through the keyword-argument constructor, paying keyword dispatch and a redundant DateTime(epoch) conversion for every time sample iterated.** (in `Base.:(:)(start::ITime, step::ITime, stop::ITime)`) **Mechanism:** Inside the returned generator, `ITime(count, period = start.period, epoch = common_epoch)` goes through the keyword constructor (line 56) on every iteration, re-running the `isnothing(epoch) || (epoch = Dates.DateTime(epoch))` branch and keyword machinery, whereas the positional 3-arg inner constructor avoids both. **Fix:** Drop the keyword form and use the positional inner constructor: `ITime(count, start.period, common_epoch)` (common_epoch is Nothing or DateTime, so no conversion is lost).
- **`src/ITime.jl:349`** _(impact: low)_ — **mod builds its result through the keyword-argument constructor, paying keyword dispatch plus a redundant DateTime(epoch) conversion on a per-timestep path.** (in `Base.mod(x::ITime, y::ITime)`) **Mechanism:** `ITime(reminder, period = x.period, epoch = x.epoch)` routes through the keyword constructor at ITime.jl:56, which unconditionally executes `isnothing(epoch) || (epoch = Dates.DateTime(epoch))` (a no-op DateTime(DateTime) identity call) and keyword-arg processing, instead of hitting the 3-arg positional inner constructor directly the way the sibling +/- ops do (macro-generated line 434). **Fix:** Use the positional inner constructor: `return ITime(reminder, x.period, x.epoch)`. It is exactly equivalent because x.epoch is always Nothing or DateTime (the struct's EPOCH parameter is `Union{Nothing, Dates.DateTime}`), so the dropped DateTime conversion is a no-op. **⚠️ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5d): REAL (sibling-asymmetry) but negligible.** Confirmed `mod` (L349) builds its result via the keyword constructor (ITime.jl:56) while the macro-generated `+`/`-` (L434) use the 3-arg positional inner constructor directly — a genuine inconsistency, and `x.epoch` is always Nothing/DateTime so `ITime(reminder, x.period, x.epoch)` is equivalent. BUT the overhead (keyword dispatch + a no-op `isnothing` check) is tiny and likely optimized away, and I could not confirm `mod(::ITime,::ITime)` is on a per-timestep path. Apply as a 1-line consistency cleanup, not for measurable speedup. Same reasoning for the range constructor at ITime.jl:335.
- **`src/Utils.jl:227`** _(impact: low)_ — **endofperiod's Month branch extracts `month(date)` twice, recomputing the same value on a per-step (and per-element) hot path.** (in `endofperiod`) **Mechanism:** The Month branch calls `month(date)` once for the `== 12` test (line 227) and again for `month(date) + 1` (line 230); the value is invariant within the call, so it can be computed once and reused (the 'same lookup done twice' pattern). **Fix:** Hoist the lookup: `elseif period isa Month\n m = month(date)\n if m == 12\n return DateTime(year(date), 12, 31, 23, 59, 59)\n else\n return DateTime(year(date), m + 1, 1) - Second(1)\n end` **✅ Verified (adversarial, vs source — Pass 5f): REAL, low.** Confirmed the Month `else` branch computes `month(date)` twice (L227 test + L230 `+1`); hoisting to `m = month(date)` computes it once. `endofperiod` is called per-element inside the LPF filters (L247/253), so there's a tiny multiplicative benefit — but `month()` is cheap, so impact is low.

---

## Pass 6 — all-dimension whole-repo sweep, performance-weighted (verified)

A fresh whole-repo audit (53 finders: perf-focused type-stability/dispatch/allocation/fusion lenses per hot file + all-dimension catch-all + 5 cross-file specialists), deduped against Passes 1–5, each candidate adversarially verified. **Completed: 267 agents, 0 errors, 8.35M tokens; 71 unique findings survived** (25 perf; 35 high-confidence). No Julia executed. ⚠️ Several verifier notes flag that a finding's *headline fix* is unsound as written (e.g. the ITime same-type comparison fast-path needs a runtime period/epoch guard) — read the per-item verifier notes before applying.

### SRC (Pass 6)

#### `src/ClimaArtifacts.jl`
- **L28** `[doc-error/docstring-vs-code semantics]` _(impact: low, effort: easy)_ — The docstring claims the returned path 'is always a folder', but the macro supports slash-indexing into an artifact, in which case the returned path is a file, not a folder. **Fix:** Reword the docstring to acknowledge slash-indexing, e.g. 'Return the path of the given artifact. Without slash-indexing this is a folder (Julia artifacts can contain multiple files); with slash-indexing ("name/sub/path") it is the path to the indexed file or subfolder inside the artifact.'

#### `src/ClimaUtilities.jl`
- **L1** `[style/documentation consistency / API discoverability]` _(impact: low, effort: easy)_ — The top-level `ClimaUtilities` module has no docstring, unlike every submodule it aggregates. **Fix:** Add a `"""ClimaUtilities\n\n<summary + pointers to submodules>\n"""` docstring block immediately above `module ClimaUtilities`.

#### `src/DataStructures.jl`
- **L93** `[bug/error-handling / resource-consistency / reentrancy]` _(impact: low, effort: moderate)_ — Both `get!` forms mutate `priority` and evict via `_enforce_size!` BEFORE the value is obtained, so if the `default`/do-block throws (or is reentrant) `priority` and `cache.cache` desync and a valid entry is evicted for a key that is never stored. **Fix:** Do the recency/eviction bookkeeping AFTER the value is successfully produced. E.g. compute `haskey(cache.cache,key)` first; if hit, `_update_priority!` + return; on miss, call `default()`/insert into `cache.cache` first, and only then `_update_priority!(cache,key)` and `_enforce_size!(cache)`. This…
- **L88** `[perf/dispatch]` _(impact: low, effort: easy)_ — The do-block get! declares `default::Callable` without a `where {F}` type parameter and only FORWARDS it (never calls it directly), so Julia's specialization-avoidance heuristic for Function/Type-typed args may skip specializing this wrapper, turning the forwarding call into a runtime dynamic dispatch on every invocation. **Fix:** Force specialization with a type parameter: `function Base.get!(default::F, cache::LRUCache{K,V}, key::K) where {F<:Callable, K, V}`. Zero-risk, standard pattern for higher-order wrappers; guarantees the forwarding call is statically dispatched.
- **L290** `[doc-error/docstring-vs-code semantics]` _(impact: low, effort: easy)_ — `_enforce_size!` docstring says it removes items "until its size is less than or equal to max_size" (a loop), but the body uses a single `if`, so it evicts at most ONE entry per call. **Fix:** Either change `if` to `while length(cache.priority) > cache.max_size`, matching the documented "until" semantics, or fix the docstring to say it removes a single least-recently-used item.
- **L60** `[style/API consistency (AbstractDict/Dict substitutability)]` _(impact: low, effort: moderate)_ — The tight `key::K`/`value::V`/`default::V` signatures reject convertible arguments that a plain `Dict` accepts, so an LRUCache is not a drop-in AbstractDict for convertible key/value types. **Fix:** Loosen the signatures to accept `key` and `value`/`default` generically and `convert(K,key)`/`convert(V,value)` internally (mirroring Dict), or at minimum document that keys/values must already be of the exact stored types.
